<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Privacy</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Seclusion from unwanted attention</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Private information" redirects here. For personally identifying information, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personal_information" title="Personal information">Personal information</a>. For information transmitted privately, see <a href="/wiki/Secrecy" title="Secrecy">Secrecy</a>. For the 1952 film, see <a href="/wiki/Private_Information" title="Private Information"><i>Private Information</i></a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Privacy_(disambiguation)" title="Privacy (disambiguation)">Privacy (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bansky_one_nation_under_cctv.jpg"><img alt="see caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="535" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Bansky_one_nation_under_cctv.jpg/330px-Bansky_one_nation_under_cctv.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Bansky_one_nation_under_cctv.jpg/495px-Bansky_one_nation_under_cctv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Bansky_one_nation_under_cctv.jpg/660px-Bansky_one_nation_under_cctv.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bansky_one_nation_under_cctv.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Banksy" title="Banksy">Banksy</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/One_Nation_Under_CCTV" title="One Nation Under CCTV">One Nation Under CCTV</a></i> graffiti, adjacent to an actual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CCTV" title="CCTV">CCTV</a> camera</div></div></div>
<p><b>Privacy</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">UK</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">US</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span></span>-/</a></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> is the ability of an individual or group to seclude themselves or information about themselves, and thereby express themselves selectively.
</p><p>The domain of privacy partially overlaps with <a href="/wiki/Security" title="Security">security</a>, which can include the concepts of appropriate use and protection of information. Privacy may also take the form of <a href="/wiki/Bodily_integrity" title="Bodily integrity">bodily integrity</a>. The right not to be subjected to unsanctioned invasions of privacy by the <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">corporations</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Individual" title="Individual">individuals</a> is part of many countries' <a href="/wiki/Privacy_law" title="Privacy law">privacy laws</a>, and in some cases, <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitutions</a>.
</p><p>The concept of universal individual privacy is a modern concept primarily associated with <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Culture" title="British Culture">British</a> and North American, and remained virtually unknown in some cultures until recent times. Now, most cultures recognize the ability of individuals to withhold certain parts of personal information from wider society. With the rise of technology, the debate regarding privacy has shifted from a bodily sense to a digital sense. As the world has become digital, there have been conflicts regarding the legal right to privacy and where it is applicable. In most countries, the right to a reasonable expectation to digital privacy has been extended from the original right to privacy, and many countries, notably the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">US</a>, under its agency, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a>, and those within the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU), have passed acts that further protect digital privacy from public and private entities and grant additional rights to users of technology.
</p><p>With the rise of the Internet, there has been an increase in the prevalence of <a href="/wiki/Social_bot" title="Social bot">social bots</a>, causing political polarization and harassment. Online harassment has also spiked, particularly with teenagers, which has consequently resulted in multiple privacy breaches. Selfie culture, the prominence of networks like <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> and <a href="/wiki/Instagram" title="Instagram">Instagram</a>, location technology, and the use of advertisements and their tracking methods also pose threats to digital privacy.
</p><p>Through the rise of technology and immensity of the debate regarding privacy, there have been various conceptions of privacy, which include the right to be let alone as defined in "<a href="/wiki/The_Right_to_Privacy_(article)" title="The Right to Privacy (article)">The Right to Privacy</a>", the first U.S. publication discussing privacy as a legal right, to the theory of the privacy paradox, which describes the notion that users' online may say they are concerned about their privacy, but in reality, are not. Along with various understandings of privacy, there are actions that reduce privacy, the most recent classification includes processing of information, sharing information, and invading personal space to get private information, as defined by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_J._Solove" title="Daniel J. Solove">Daniel J. Solove</a>. Conversely, in order to protect a user's privacy, multiple steps can be taken, specifically through practicing <a href="/wiki/Encryption" title="Encryption">encryption</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anonymity" title="Anonymity">anonymity</a>, and taking further measures to bolster the security of their data.
</p>

</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-58"><a href="#Privacy_in_non-human_animals"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Privacy in non-human animals</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Privacy_law" title="Privacy law">Privacy law</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Ladies%27_home_journal_(1948)_(14787773943).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4637" data-file-width="3405" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/The_Ladies%27_home_journal_%281948%29_%2814787773943%29.jpg/220px-The_Ladies%27_home_journal_%281948%29_%2814787773943%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/The_Ladies%27_home_journal_%281948%29_%2814787773943%29.jpg/330px-The_Ladies%27_home_journal_%281948%29_%2814787773943%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/The_Ladies%27_home_journal_%281948%29_%2814787773943%29.jpg/440px-The_Ladies%27_home_journal_%281948%29_%2814787773943%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Ladies%27_home_journal_(1948)_(14787773943).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Advertisement with a highlighted quote <a href="/wiki/Blushing" title="Blushing">"my face got redder and redder!"</a> There is a highlighted quote on the importance of being honest with oneself, and after two and a half pages concludes with a suspicion that telephone operators are listening in on every call.</div></div></div>
<p>Privacy has historical roots in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek philosophical discussions</a>. The most well-known of these was Aristotle's distinction between two spheres of life: the public sphere of the <i><a href="/wiki/Polis" title="Polis">polis</a></i>, associated with political life, and the private sphere of the <i><a href="/wiki/Oikos" title="Oikos">oikos</a></i>, associated with domestic life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-3">[3]</a></sup> In the United States, more systematic treatises of privacy did not appear until the 1890s, with the development of <a href="/wiki/Privacy_laws_of_the_United_States" title="Privacy laws of the United States">privacy law in America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology">Technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="#Techniques_to_improve_privacy">§ Techniques to improve privacy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Privacy-enhancing_technologies" title="Privacy-enhancing technologies">Privacy-enhancing technologies</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IllinoisTelephoneAndTelegraphAd.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1413" data-file-width="933" decoding="async" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/IllinoisTelephoneAndTelegraphAd.png/200px-IllinoisTelephoneAndTelegraphAd.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/IllinoisTelephoneAndTelegraphAd.png/300px-IllinoisTelephoneAndTelegraphAd.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/IllinoisTelephoneAndTelegraphAd.png/400px-IllinoisTelephoneAndTelegraphAd.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IllinoisTelephoneAndTelegraphAd.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Advertisement for dial telephone service available to delegates to the 1912 <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> convention in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>. A major selling point of dial telephone service was that it was "secret", in that no operator was required to connect the call.</div></div></div>
<p>As technology has advanced, the way in which privacy is protected and violated has changed with it. In the case of some technologies, such as the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>, the increased ability to share information can lead to new ways in which privacy can be breached. It is generally agreed that the first publication advocating privacy in the United States was the 1890 article by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_D._Warren_(US_attorney)" title="Samuel D. Warren (US attorney)">Samuel Warren</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Brandeis" title="Louis Brandeis">Louis Brandeis</a>, "The Right to Privacy",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and that it was written mainly in response to the increase in newspapers and photographs made possible by printing technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1948, <i><a href="/wiki/Nineteen_Eighty-Four" title="Nineteen Eighty-Four">1984</a>,</i> written by <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a>, was published. A classic dystopian novel, <i>1984</i> describes the life of Winston Smith in 1984, located in Oceania, a totalitarian state. The all-controlling Party, the party in power led by Big Brother, is able to control power through mass <a href="/wiki/Surveillance" title="Surveillance">surveillance</a> and limited freedom of speech and thought. George Orwell provides commentary on the negative effects of <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarianism</a>, particularly on privacy and <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censorship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Parallels have been drawn between <i>1984</i> and modern censorship and privacy, a notable example being that large social media companies, rather than the government, are able to monitor a user's data and decide what is allowed to be said online through their censorship policies, ultimately for monetary purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1960s, people began to consider how changes in technology were bringing changes in the concept of privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vance_Packard" title="Vance Packard">Vance Packard</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Naked_Society" title="The Naked Society">The Naked Society</a></i> was a popular book on privacy from that era and led US discourse on privacy at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4-8">[8]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Alan_F._Westin" title="Alan F. Westin">Alan Westin</a>'s <i>Privacy and Freedom</i> shifted the debate regarding privacy from a physical sense, how the government controls a person's body (i.e. <i><a href="/wiki/Roe_v._Wade" title="Roe v. Wade">Roe v. Wade</a></i>) and other activities such as wiretapping and photography. As important records became digitized, Westin argued that personal data was becoming too accessible and that a person should have complete jurisdiction over their data, laying the foundation for the modern discussion of privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>New technologies can also create new ways to gather private information. For example, in the United States, it was thought that heat sensors intended to be used to find marijuana-growing operations would be acceptable. Contrary to popular opinion, in 2001 in <i><a href="/wiki/Kyllo_v._United_States" title="Kyllo v. United States">Kyllo v. United States</a></i> (533 U.S. 27) it was decided that the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_imaging" title="Thermal imaging">thermal imaging</a> devices that can reveal previously unknown information without a warrant does indeed constitute a violation of privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> In 2019, after developing a corporate rivalry in competing voice-recognition software, <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amazon_(company)" title="Amazon (company)">Amazon</a> required employees to listen to <a href="#Intimacy">intimate</a> moments and faithfully transcribe the contents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloomberg-siri-alexa-listen_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg-siri-alexa-listen-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Police_and_government">Police and government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Police and government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Police" title="Police">Police</a>, <a href="/wiki/Police_body_camera" title="Police body camera">Police body camera</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PRISM_(surveillance_program)" title="PRISM (surveillance program)">PRISM (surveillance program)</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/The_Wire" title="The Wire">The Wire</a></div><p>Police and citizens often conflict on what degree the police can intrude a citizen's digital privacy. For instance, in 2012, the Supreme Court ruled unanimously in <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Jones" title="United States v. Jones">United States v. Jones</a></i> (565 U.S. 400), in the case of Antoine Jones who was arrested of drug possession using a <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">GPS</a> tracker on his car that was placed without a warrant, that warrantless tracking infringes the Fourth Amendment. The Supreme Court also justified that there is some "reasonable expectation of privacy" in transportation since the reasonable expectation of privacy had already been established under <i>Griswold v. Connecticut</i> (1965). The Supreme Court also further clarified that the Fourth Amendment did not only pertain to physical instances of intrusion but also digital instances, and thus <i>United States v. Jones</i> became a landmark case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, the Supreme Court ruled unanimously in <i><a href="/wiki/Riley_v._California" title="Riley v. California">Riley v. California</a></i> (573 U.S. 373), where David Leon Riley was arrested after he was pulled over for driving on expired license tags when the police searched his phone and discovered that he was tied to a shooting, that searching a citizen's phone without a warrant was an unreasonable search, a violation of the Fourth Amendment. The Supreme Court concluded that the cell phones contained personal information different than trivial items, and went beyond to state that information stored on the cloud was not necessarily a form of evidence. <i>Riley v. California</i> evidently became a landmark case, protecting the digital protection of citizen's privacy when confronted with the police.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>A recent notable occurrence of the conflict between law enforcement and a citizen in terms of digital privacy has been in the 2018 case, <i><a href="/wiki/Carpenter_v._United_States" title="Carpenter v. United States">Carpenter v. United States</a></i> (585 U.S. ____). In this case, the FBI used cell phone records without a warrant to arrest Timothy Ivory Carpenter on multiple charges, and the Supreme Court ruled that the warrantless search of cell phone records violated the Fourth Amendment, citing that the Fourth Amendment protects "reasonable expectations of privacy" and that information sent to third parties still falls under data that can be included under "reasonable expectations of privacy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beyond law enforcement, many interactions between the government and citizens have been revealed either lawfully or unlawfully, specifically through whistleblowers. One notable example is <a href="/wiki/Edward_Snowden" title="Edward Snowden">Edward Snowden</a>, who released multiple operations related to the mass surveillance operations of the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> (NSA), where it was discovered that the NSA continues to breach the security of millions of people, mainly through mass surveillance programs whether it was collecting great amounts of data through third party private companies, hacking into other embassies or frameworks of international countries, and various breaches of data, which prompted a culture shock and stirred international debate related to digital privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Internet">Internet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Internet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Internet_privacy" title="Internet privacy">Internet privacy</a></div>
<p>In the European Union (EU), personal data, in whatever form it is received, data protection rights must be respected by law. This also applies to companies based outside of the EU which conduct business inside the EU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, the United States has disparate state and federal laws governing Internet privacy, with much left unregulated. While data privacy protection laws can grant individuals rights to control their data, enforcement is increasingly complex. A variety of data may be covered by specific laws, such as  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIPAA" title="HIPAA">HIPAA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fair_Credit_Reporting_Act" title="Fair Credit Reporting Act">FCRA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FERPA" title="FERPA">FERPA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GLBA" title="GLBA">GLBA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ECPA" title="ECPA">ECPA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COPPA" title="COPPA">COPPA</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VPPA" title="VPPA">VPPA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_discussions_of_Internet_privacy">Legal discussions of Internet privacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Legal discussions of Internet privacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="#Police_and_Government">§ Police and Government</a>, <a href="#Legal_right_to_privacy">§ Legal right to privacy</a>, and <a href="#Ethical_Controversies_Over_Location_Privacy">§ Ethical Controversies Over Location Privacy</a></div><p>The Internet has brought new concerns about privacy in an age where computers can permanently store records of everything: "where every online photo, status update, Twitter post and blog entry by and about us can be stored forever", writes law professor and author <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Rosen_(legal_academic)" title="Jeffrey Rosen (legal academic)">Jeffrey Rosen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes1-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the first instances of privacy being discussed in a legal manner was in 1914, the Federal Trade Commission (FTC) was established, under the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission_Act_of_1914" title="Federal Trade Commission Act of 1914">Federal Trade Commission Act</a>, whose initial goal was to promote competition amongst businesses and prohibit unfair and misleading businesses. However, since the 1970s, the FTC has become involved in privacy law and enforcement, the first instance being the FTC's implementation and enforcement of the <a href="/wiki/Fair_Credit_Reporting_Act" title="Fair Credit Reporting Act">Fair Credit Reporting Act</a> (FCRA), which regulates how credit card bureaus can use a client's data and grants consumer's further credit card rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In addition to the FCRA, the FTC has implemented various other important acts that protect consumer privacy. For example, the FTC passed the <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Online_Privacy_Protection_Act" title="Children's Online Privacy Protection Act">Children's Online Privacy Protection Act</a> (COPPA) of 1998, which regulates services geared towards children under the age of thirteen, and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Flags_Rule" title="Red Flags Rule">Red Flags Rule</a>, passed in 2010, which warrants that companies have measures to protect clients against <a href="/wiki/Identity_theft" title="Identity theft">identity theft</a>, and if clients become victims of identity theft, that there are steps to alleviate the consequences of identity theft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2018, the European Union (EU)'s General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) went into effect, a privacy legislation that replaced the Data Protection Directive of 1995. The GDPR requires how consumers within the EU must have complete and concise knowledge about how companies use their data and have the right to gain and correct data that a companies stores regarding them, enforcing stricter privacy legislations compared to the Data Protection Directive of 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_networking">Social networking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Social networking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Privacy_concerns_with_social_networking_services" title="Privacy concerns with social networking services">Privacy concerns with social networking services</a></div>
<p>Several online social network sites (OSNs) are among the top 10 most visited websites globally. Facebook for example, as of August 2015, was the largest social-networking site, with nearly 2.7 billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> members, who upload over 4.75 billion pieces of content daily. While <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> is significantly smaller with 316 million registered users, the US <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> recently announced that it will be acquiring and permanently storing the entire archive of public Twitter posts since 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes1-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>A review and evaluation of scholarly work regarding the current state of the value of individuals' privacy of online social networking show the following results: "first, adults seem to be more concerned about potential privacy threats than younger users; second, policy makers should be alarmed by a large part of users who underestimate risks of their information privacy on OSNs; third, in the case of using OSNs and its services, traditional one-dimensional privacy approaches fall short".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This is exacerbated by <a href="/wiki/Data_re-identification" title="Data re-identification">deanonymization</a> research indicating that personal traits such as sexual orientation, race, religious and political views, personality, or intelligence can be inferred based on a wide variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_footprints" title="Digital footprints">digital footprints</a>, such as samples of text, browsing logs, or Facebook Likes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kosinski_2013_5802–5805_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kosinski_2013_5802–5805-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Intrusions of social media privacy are known to affect employment in the United States. <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> reports that 75 percent of U.S. recruiters and human-resource professionals now do online research about candidates, often using information provided by search engines, social-networking sites, photo/video-sharing sites, personal web sites and blogs, and <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>. They also report that 70 percent of U.S. recruiters have rejected candidates based on internet information. This has created a need by many candidates to control various online privacy settings in addition to controlling their online reputations, the conjunction of which has led to legal suits against both social media sites and US employers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes1-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Selfie_culture">Selfie culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Selfie culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h6>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="#Privacy_paradox_and_economic_valuation">§ Privacy paradox and economic valuation</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selfies" title="Selfies">Selfies</a> are popular today. A search for photos with the hashtag #selfie retrieves over 23 million results on Instagram and 51 million with the hashtag #me.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> However, due to modern corporate and governmental surveillance, this may pose a risk to privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In a research study which takes a sample size of 3763, researchers found that for users posting selfies on social media, women generally have greater concerns over privacy than men, and that users' privacy concerns inversely predict their selfie behavior and activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Online_harassment">Online harassment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Online harassment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Online_harassment" title="Online harassment">Online harassment</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doxxing" title="Doxxing">Doxxing</a></div><p>After the 1999 <a href="/wiki/Columbine_High_School_massacre" title="Columbine High School massacre">Columbine Shooting</a>, where violent video games and music were thought to be one of the main influences on the killers, some states began to pass anti-bullying laws where some included cyber-bullying laws. The suicide of 13-year-old <a href="/wiki/Suicide_of_Megan_Meier" title="Suicide of Megan Meier">Megan Meier</a>, where Meier was harassed on <a href="/wiki/Myspace" title="Myspace">Myspace</a>, prompted Missouri to pass anti-harassment laws though the perpetrators were later declared innocent. Through the rise of smartphones and the rise in popularity with social media such as Facebook and Instagram, messaging, online forums, gaming communities, and email, online harassment continued to grow. 18-year-old Jessica Logan committed suicide in 2009 after her boyfriend sent explicit photos of her to various teenagers in  different high schools, where she was then harassed through Myspace, which led to her school passing anti-harassment laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-30">[30]</a></sup> Further notable occurrences where digital privacy was invaded include the death of <a href="/wiki/Suicide_of_Tyler_Clementi" title="Suicide of Tyler Clementi">Tyler Clementi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suicide_of_Amanda_Todd" title="Suicide of Amanda Todd">Amanda Todd</a>, whose death instigated Canadian funding towards studies on bullying and legislation regarding cyber-bullying to be passed, but problems regarding the lack of protection for users were risen, by Todd's mother herself, since this bill allowed for companies to completely access a user's data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2013_Bill_C-13_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-2013_Bill_C-13-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Justice_Committee_on_May_13th,_2014_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Justice_Committee_on_May_13th,_2014-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>All U.S. states have now passed <a href="/wiki/Cyberbullying" title="Cyberbullying">laws regarding online harassment</a>. 15% of adolescents aged 12–18 have been subject to cyberbullying, according to a 2017 report conducted by the National Center for Education Statistics and Bureau of Justice. In 2018 16% of high schoolers were subject to cyberbullying according to the CDC's 2019 Youth Risk Behavior Surveillance System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Bot_accounts">Bot accounts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Bot accounts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_bot" title="Social bot">Social bot</a></div>
<p>Bots originate from the 1980s, where they were known as an <a href="/wiki/Internet_Relay_Chat" title="Internet Relay Chat">IRC</a> (Internet Relayed Chat) which served essential purposes such as stating the date and time and over time now expand to other purposes, such as flagging copyright on articles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Forms of social media, such as Twitter, Facebook, and Instagram, have a prevalent activity of social bots, different than IRC bots, representing accounts that are not human and perform autonomous behavior to some degree. Bots, especially those with malicious intent, became most prevalent in the 2016 U.S. presidential election, where both the Trump and Clinton campaign had millions of bots essentially working on their account to influence the election. A subsection of these bots would target and assault certain journalists, causing journalists to stop reporting on matters since they dreaded further harassment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>  In the same election, Russian Twitter bots, presenting themselves as Midwestern swing-voter Republicans were used to amplify and spread misinformation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In October 2020, data scientist Emilio Ferrara found that 19% of tweets related to the 2016 election were generated by bots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Following the election, A 2017 study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> revealed that nearly 48 million Twitter accounts are bots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Furthermore, it was found that approximately 33% of tweets related to <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">Brexit</a> were produced by bots. Data indicates that the use of bots has increased since the 2016 election, and AI-driven bots are becoming hard to detect, and soon will be able to emulate human-like behavior, by being able to comment, submit comments regarding policy, etc. affecting political debate, outcomes of an election, and a user's perception of those online and the humans they interact with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although bots have been used in a negative context when politics are described, many bots have been used to protect against online harassment. For example, since 2020, Facebook researchers have been developing Web-Enabled Simulation (WES) bots that emulate bad human behavior and then engineers use this data to determine the best correctives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Privacy_and_location-based_services">Privacy and location-based services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Privacy and location-based services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Location-based_service" title="Location-based service">Location-based service</a></div>
<p>Increasingly, mobile devices facilitate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geolocation" title="Geolocation">location tracking</a>.  This creates user privacy problems.  A user's location and preferences constitute personal information.  Their improper use violates that user's privacy.  A recent MIT study by de Montjoye et al. showed that 4 spatio-temporal points, approximate places and times, are enough to uniquely identify 95% of 1.5M people in a mobility database. The study
further shows that these constraints hold even when the resolution of the dataset is low. Therefore, even coarse or blurred datasets provide little anonymity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several methods to protect user privacy in <a href="/wiki/Location-based_service" title="Location-based service">location-based services</a> have been proposed, including the use of anonymizing servers and blurring of information. Methods to quantify privacy have also been proposed, to calculate the equilibrium between the benefit of providing accurate location information and the drawbacks of risking personal privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Advertising_on_mobile_devices">Advertising on mobile devices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Advertising on mobile devices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>When the internet was first introduced, the internet became the predominant medium of advertising, shifting from newspapers and magazines. With the growth of digital advertisements, people began to be tracked using <a href="/wiki/HTTP_cookie" title="HTTP cookie">HTTP cookies</a>, and this data was used to target relevant audiences. Since the introduction of iPhones and Androids, data brokers were also planted within apps, for further tracking. Since the growth of cookies, resulting in a $350 billion digital industry especially focused on mobile devices, digital privacy has become the main source of concern for many mobile users, especially with the rise of privacy scandals, such as the <a href="/wiki/Facebook%E2%80%93Cambridge_Analytica_data_scandal" title="Facebook–Cambridge Analytica data scandal">Cambridge Analytica Scandal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-45">[45]</a></sup> Recently, <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a> has introduced features that prohibit advertisers from tracking a user's data without their consent, as seen with their implementation of pop-up notifications that let users decide the extent to which a company can track their behavior. <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> has begun to roll out similar features, but concerns have risen about how a privacy-conscious internet will function without advertisers being able to use data from users as a form of capital. Apple has set a precedent implementing stricter crackdown on privacy, especially with their introduction of the pop-up feature, which has made it harder for businesses, especially small businesses, on other mediums, like Facebook to target relevant audiences, since these advertisers no longer have relevant data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Google, contrary to Apple, has remained relatively lax in its crackdown, supporting cookies until at least 2023, until a privacy-conscious internet solution is found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethical_controversies_over_location_privacy">Ethical controversies over location privacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ethical controversies over location privacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>There have been scandals regarding location privacy. One instance was the scandal concerning <a href="/wiki/AccuWeather" title="AccuWeather">AccuWeather</a>, where it was revealed that AccuWeather was selling locational data. This consisted of a user's locational data, even if they opted out within Accuweather, which tracked users’ location. Accuweather sold this data to Reveal Mobile, a company that monetizes data related to a user's location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>  Other international cases are similar to the Accuweather case. In 2017, a leaky API inside the McDelivery App exposed private data, which consisted of home addresses, of 2.2 million users <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>  With the rise of such scandals, many large American technology companies such as Google, Apple, and Facebook have been subjected to hearings and pressure under the U.S. legislative system. In 2011, with the rise of locational technology, US Senator <a href="/wiki/Al_Franken" title="Al Franken">Al Franken</a> wrote an open letter to <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jobs" title="Steve Jobs">Steve Jobs</a>, noting the ability of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPhones" title="IPhones">iPhones</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPads" title="IPads">iPads</a> to record and store users' locations in unencrypted files,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>  although Apple denied doing so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>  This conflict has perpetuated further into 2021; a recent example being where the U.S. state of Arizona found in a court case that Google mislead its users and stored the location of users regardless of their location settings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-state-arizona-redacted-complaint_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-state-arizona-redacted-complaint-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metadata">Metadata</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Metadata">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metadata" title="Metadata">Metadata</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Data_re-identification" title="Data re-identification">Data re-identification</a></div>
<p>The ability to do online inquiries about individuals has expanded dramatically over the last decade. Importantly, directly observed behavior, such as browsing logs, search queries, or contents of a public Facebook profile, can be automatically processed to infer secondary information about an individual, such as sexual orientation, political and religious views, race, substance use, intelligence, and personality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Privacy_in_Australian_law#Telecommunications_(Interception_and_Access)_Amendment_(Data_Retention)_Act_2015" title="Privacy in Australian law">Privacy in Australian law § Telecommunications (Interception and Access) Amendment (Data Retention) Act 2015</a></div>
<p>In Australia, the <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_(Interception_and_Access)_Amendment_(Data_Retention)_Act_2015" title="Telecommunications (Interception and Access) Amendment (Data Retention) Act 2015">Telecommunications (Interception and Access) Amendment (Data Retention) Act 2015</a> made a distinction between collecting the contents of messages sent between users and the metadata surrounding those messages.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Protection_of_privacy_on_the_Internet">Protection of privacy on the Internet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Protection of privacy on the Internet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="#Actions_to_improve_privacy">§ Actions to improve privacy</a></div>
<p>Covert collection of personally identifiable information has been identified as a primary concern by the U.S. Federal Trade Commission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FederalTrade_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FederalTrade-54">[54]</a></sup> Although some privacy advocates recommend the deletion of original and third-party HTTP cookies, Anthony Miyazaki, marketing professor at <a href="/wiki/Florida_International_University" title="Florida International University">Florida International University</a> and privacy scholar, warns that the "elimination of third-party cookie use by Web sites can be circumvented by cooperative strategies with third parties in which information is transferred after the Web site's use of original domain cookies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> As of December 2010, the Federal Trade Commission is reviewing policy regarding this issue as it relates to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behavioral_advertising" title="Behavioral advertising">behavioral advertising</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FederalTrade_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FederalTrade-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_right_to_privacy">Legal right to privacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Legal right to privacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_privacy" title="Right to privacy">Right to privacy</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Privacy_law" title="Privacy law">Privacy law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information_privacy_law" title="Information privacy law">Information privacy law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Right_to_be_forgotten" title="Right to be forgotten">Right to be forgotten</a>, and <a href="#Legal_discussions_of_Internet_privacy">§ Legal discussions of Internet privacy</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Privacy_International_2007_privacy_ranking_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1357" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Privacy_International_2007_privacy_ranking_map.png/300px-Privacy_International_2007_privacy_ranking_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Privacy_International_2007_privacy_ranking_map.png/450px-Privacy_International_2007_privacy_ranking_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Privacy_International_2007_privacy_ranking_map.png/600px-Privacy_International_2007_privacy_ranking_map.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Privacy_International_2007_privacy_ranking_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Privacy_International" title="Privacy International">Privacy International</a> 2007 privacy ranking. On one end of the spectrum, green indicates countries that uphold human rights standards while on the other end, red indicates countries considered endemic surveillance societies. This ranking was the last global report conducted by Privacy International, and it is demonstrated that countries that do have the legal right to privacy explicitly mentioned in their constitutions trend closer to yellow and green while those that do not trend closer to red.</div></div></div>
<p>Most countries give citizens rights to privacy in their constitutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4-8">[8]</a></sup> Representative examples of this include the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Brazil" title="Constitution of Brazil">Constitution of Brazil</a>, which says "the privacy, private life, honor and image of people are inviolable"; the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_South_Africa" title="Constitution of South Africa">Constitution of South Africa</a> says that "everyone has a right to privacy"; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Republic_of_Korea" title="Constitution of the Republic of Korea">Constitution of the Republic of Korea</a> says "the privacy of no citizen shall be infringed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4-8">[8]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Constitution" title="Italian Constitution">Italian Constitution</a> also defines the right to privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Among most countries whose constitutions do not explicitly describe privacy rights, court decisions have interpreted their constitutions to intend to give privacy rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Japan#Other_issues" title="Human rights in Japan">Human rights in Japan § Other issues</a></div>
<p>Many countries have broad privacy laws outside their constitutions, including Australia's <a href="/wiki/Privacy_Act_1988" title="Privacy Act 1988">Privacy Act 1988</a>, Argentina's Law for the Protection of Personal Data of 2000, Canada's 2000 <a href="/wiki/Personal_Information_Protection_and_Electronic_Documents_Act" title="Personal Information Protection and Electronic Documents Act">Personal Information Protection and Electronic Documents Act</a>, and Japan's 2003 Personal Information Protection Law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beyond national privacy laws, there are international privacy agreements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove20103_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove20103-57">[57]</a></sup> The United Nations <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> says "No one shall be subjected to arbitrary interference with [their] privacy, family, home or correspondence, nor to attacks upon [their] honor and reputation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4-8">[8]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a> published its Privacy Guidelines in 1980. The European Union's 1995 Data Protection Directive guides privacy protection in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4-8">[8]</a></sup> The 2004 Privacy Framework by the <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific_Economic_Cooperation" title="Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation">Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation</a> is a privacy protection agreement for the members of that organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4_8-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove20103–4-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Argument_against_legal_protection_of_privacy">Argument against legal protection of privacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Argument against legal protection of privacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The argument against the legal protection of privacy is predominant in the US. The landmark US Supreme Court case, <i>Griswold v. Connecticut,</i> established a reasonable expectation to privacy. However, some conservative justices do not consider privacy to be a legal right, as when discussing the 2003 case, <i><a href="/wiki/Lawrence_v._Texas" title="Lawrence v. Texas">Lawrence v. Texas</a></i> (539 U.S. 558), Supreme Court Justice <a href="/wiki/Antonin_Scalia" title="Antonin Scalia">Antonin Scalia</a> did not consider privacy to be a right, and Supreme Court Justice <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Thomas" title="Clarence Thomas">Clarence Thomas</a> argued that there is "no general right to privacy" in the U.S. Constitution in 2007. Many Republican interest groups and activists desire for appointed justices to be like Justice Thomas and Scalia since they uphold <a href="/wiki/Originalism" title="Originalism">originalism</a>, which indirectly helps strengthen the argument against the legal protection of privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_market_vs_consumer_protection">Free market vs consumer protection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Free market vs consumer protection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="#Conceptions_of_Privacy">§ Conceptions of Privacy</a></div>
<p>Approaches to privacy can, broadly, be divided into two categories: free market or consumer protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quinn_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-quinn-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>One example of the free market approach is to be found in the voluntary OECD Guidelines on the Protection of Privacy and Transborder Flows of Personal Data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The principles reflected in the guidelines, free of legislative interference, are analyzed in an article putting them into perspective with concepts of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDPR" title="GDPR">GDPR</a> put into law later in the European Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a consumer protection approach, in contrast, it is claimed that individuals may not have the time or knowledge to make informed choices, or may not have reasonable alternatives available. In support of this view, Jensen and Potts showed that most <a href="/wiki/Privacy_policy" title="Privacy policy">privacy policies</a> are above the reading level of the average person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jensen_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="By_country">By country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: By country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Privacy_in_Australian_law" title="Privacy in Australian law">Privacy in Australian law</a></div>
<p>The <i>Privacy Act 1988</i> is administered by the Office of the Australian Information Commissioner. The initial introduction of privacy law in 1998 extended to the public sector, specifically to Federal government departments, under the Information Privacy Principles. State government agencies can also be subject to state based privacy legislation. This built upon the already existing privacy requirements that applied to telecommunications providers (under Part 13 of the <i>Telecommunications Act 1997</i>), and confidentiality requirements that already applied to banking, legal and patient / doctor relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008 the Australian Law Reform Commission (ALRC) conducted a review of Australian privacy law and produced a report titled "For Your Information".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Recommendations were taken up and implemented by the Australian Government via the Privacy Amendment (Enhancing Privacy Protection) Bill 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Privacy_in_Australian_law#Telecommunications_(Interception_and_Access)_Amendment_(Data_Retention)_Act_2015" title="Privacy in Australian law">Privacy in Australian law § Telecommunications (Interception and Access) Amendment (Data Retention) Act 2015</a></div>
<p>In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_(Interception_and_Access)_Amendment_(Data_Retention)_Act_2015" title="Telecommunications (Interception and Access) Amendment (Data Retention) Act 2015">Telecommunications (Interception and Access) Amendment (Data Retention) Act 2015</a> was passed, to some <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_(Interception_and_Access)_Amendment_(Data_Retention)_Act_2015#Case_against_data_retention" title="Telecommunications (Interception and Access) Amendment (Data Retention) Act 2015">controversy over its human rights implications</a> and the role of media.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: European Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Information_privacy_law#Europe" title="Information privacy law">Information privacy law § Europe</a></div>
<p>Although there are comprehensive regulations for data protection in the European Union, one study finds that despite the laws, there is a lack of enforcement in that no institution feels responsible to control the parties involved and enforce their laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The European Union also champions the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_to_be_Forgotten" title="Right to be Forgotten">Right to be Forgotten</a> concept in support of its adoption by other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Since the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Aadhaar" title="Aadhaar">Aadhaar</a> project in 2009, which resulted in all 1.2 billion Indians being associated with a 12-digit biometric-secured number.  Aadhaar has uplifted the poor in India by providing them with a form of  identity and preventing the fraud and waste of resources, as normally the government would not be able to allocate its resources to its intended assignees due to the ID issues<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. With the rise of Aadhaar, India has debated whether Aadhaar violates an individual's privacy and whether any organization should have access to an individual's digital profile, as the Aadhaar card became associated with other economic sectors, allowing for the tracking of individuals by both public and private bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Aadhaar databases have suffered from security attacks as well and the project was also met with mistrust regarding the safety of the social protection infrastructures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>  In 2017, where the Aadhar was challenged, the Indian Supreme Court declared privacy as a human right, but postponed the decision regarding the constitutionality of Aadhaar for another bench.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In September 2018, the Indian Supreme Court determined that the Aadhaar project did not violate the legal right to privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Privacy_in_English_law" title="Privacy in English law">Privacy in English law</a></div>
<p>In the United Kingdom, it is not possible to bring an action for invasion of privacy. An action may be brought under another <a href="/wiki/Tort" title="Tort">tort</a> (usually breach of confidence) and privacy must then be considered under EC law. In the UK, it is sometimes a defence that disclosure of private information was in the public interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> There is, however, the <a href="/wiki/Information_Commissioner%27s_Office" title="Information Commissioner's Office">Information Commissioner's Office</a> (ICO), an independent public body set up to promote access to official information and protect personal information. They do this by promoting good practice, ruling on eligible complaints, giving information to individuals and organisations, and taking action when the law is broken. The relevant UK laws include: <a href="/wiki/Data_Protection_Act_1998" title="Data Protection Act 1998">Data Protection Act 1998</a>; <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_2000" title="Freedom of Information Act 2000">Freedom of Information Act 2000</a>; <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Information_Regulations_2004" title="Environmental Information Regulations 2004">Environmental Information Regulations 2004</a>; <a href="/wiki/Privacy_and_Electronic_Communications_(EC_Directive)_Regulations_2003" title="Privacy and Electronic Communications (EC Directive) Regulations 2003">Privacy and Electronic Communications Regulations 2003</a>. The ICO has also provided a "Personal Information Toolkit" online which explains in more detail the various ways of protecting privacy online.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Privacy_laws_of_the_United_States" title="Privacy laws of the United States">Privacy laws of the United States</a></div>
<p>Although the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">US Constitution</a> does not explicitly include the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_privacy" title="Right to privacy">right to privacy</a>, individual as well as <a href="#Privacy_and_location-based_services">locational privacy</a> may be implicitly granted by the Constitution under the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution">4th Amendment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> has found that other guarantees have <a href="/wiki/Penumbra_(law)" title="Penumbra (law)"><i>penumbras</i></a> that implicitly grant a right to privacy against government intrusion, for example in <i><a href="/wiki/Griswold_v._Connecticut" title="Griswold v. Connecticut">Griswold v. Connecticut</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Roe_v._Wade" title="Roe v. Wade">Roe v. Wade</a>.</i> <i><a href="/wiki/Dobbs_v._Jackson_Women%27s_Health_Organization" title="Dobbs v. Jackson Women's Health Organization">Dobbs v. Jackson Women's Health Organization</a></i> later overruled <i>Roe v. Wade</i>, with Supreme Court Justice <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Thomas" title="Clarence Thomas">Clarence Thomas</a> characterizing <i>Griswold</i>'s penumbral argument as having a "facial absurdity",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> casting doubt on the validity of a constitutional right to privacy in the United States and of previous decisions relying on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In the United States, the right of <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a> granted in the <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">First Amendment</a> has limited the effects of lawsuits for breach of privacy. Privacy is regulated in the US by the <a href="/wiki/Privacy_Act_of_1974" title="Privacy Act of 1974">Privacy Act of 1974</a>, and various state laws. The Privacy Act of 1974 only applies to federal agencies in the executive branch of the federal government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Certain privacy rights have been established in the United States via legislation such as the <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Online_Privacy_Protection_Act" title="Children's Online Privacy Protection Act">Children's Online Privacy Protection Act</a> (COPPA),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Gramm%E2%80%93Leach%E2%80%93Bliley_Act" title="Gramm–Leach–Bliley Act">Gramm–Leach–Bliley Act</a> (GLB), and the <a href="/wiki/Health_Insurance_Portability_and_Accountability_Act" title="Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act">Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act</a> (HIPAA).
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike the EU and most EU-member states, the US does not recognize the right to privacy of non-US citizens. The UN's Special Rapporteur on the right to privacy, Joseph A. Cannataci, criticized this distinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conceptions_of_privacy">Conceptions of privacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Conceptions of privacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Privacy_as_contextual_integrity">Privacy as contextual integrity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Privacy as contextual integrity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Contextual_integrity" title="Contextual integrity">Contextual integrity</a></div>
<p>The theory of <a href="/wiki/Contextual_integrity" title="Contextual integrity">contextual integrity</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ci_book_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-ci_book-81">[81]</a></sup> defines privacy as an appropriate information flow, where appropriateness, in turn, is defined as conformance with legitimate, informational norms specific to social contexts.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_to_be_let_alone">Right to be let alone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Right to be let alone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1890, the United States <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jurists" title="Jurists">jurists</a> Samuel D. Warren and Louis Brandeis wrote "The Right to Privacy", an article in which they argued for the "right to be let alone", using that phrase as a definition of privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201015–17_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201015–17-82">[82]</a></sup> This concept relies on the theory of <a href="/wiki/Natural_rights_and_legal_rights" title="Natural rights and legal rights">natural rights</a> and focuses on protecting individuals. The citation was a response to recent technological developments, such as photography, and sensationalist journalism, also known as <a href="/wiki/Yellow_journalism" title="Yellow journalism">yellow journalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brandeis_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brandeis-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is extensive commentary over the meaning of being "let alone", and among other ways, it has been interpreted to mean the right of a person to choose <a href="/wiki/Seclusion" title="Seclusion">seclusion</a> from the attention of others if they wish to do so, and the right to be immune from scrutiny or being observed in private settings, such as one's own home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201015–17_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201015–17-82">[82]</a></sup> Although this early vague legal concept did not describe privacy in a way that made it easy to design broad legal protections of privacy, it strengthened the notion of privacy rights for individuals and began a legacy of discussion on those rights in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201015–17_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201015–17-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Limited_access">Limited access</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Limited access">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Limited access refers to a person's ability to participate in society without having other individuals and organizations collect information about them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201019_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201019-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Various theorists have imagined privacy as a system for limiting access to one's personal information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201019_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201019-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Lawrence_Godkin" title="Edwin Lawrence Godkin">Edwin Lawrence Godkin</a> wrote in the late 19th century that "nothing is better worthy of legal protection than private life, or, in other words, the right of every man to keep his affairs to himself, and to decide for himself to what extent they shall be the subject of public observation and discussion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201019_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201019-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Adopting an approach similar to the one presented by Ruth Gavison<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transparency_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-transparency-86">[86]</a></sup> Nine years earlier,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sissela_Bok" title="Sissela Bok">Sissela Bok</a> said that privacy is "the condition of being protected from unwanted access by others—either physical access, personal information, or attention."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201019_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201019-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Control_over_information">Control over information</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Control over information">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Control over one's personal information is the concept that "privacy is the claim of individuals, groups, or institutions to determine for themselves when, how, and to what extent information about them is communicated to others." Generally, a person who has <a href="/wiki/Implied_consent" title="Implied consent">consensually formed an interpersonal relationship</a> with another person is not considered "protected" by privacy rights with respect to the person they are in the relationship with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201024_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201024-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fried" title="Charles Fried">Charles Fried</a> said that "Privacy is not simply an absence of information about us in the minds of others; rather it is the control we have over information about ourselves. Nevertheless, in the era of <a href="/wiki/Big_data" title="Big data">big data</a>, control over information is under pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Inline_citation#When_you_must_use_inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Inline citation"><span title="This reference does not contain the quote.
