(June 2021)">quote citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style#Punctuation" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style"><span title="The quote opens without closing.">check quotation syntax</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_of_privacy">States of privacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: States of privacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Alan Westin defined four states—or experiences—of privacy: solitude, intimacy, anonymity, and reserve. <a href="/wiki/Solitude" title="Solitude">Solitude</a> is a physical separation from others;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-93">[93]</a></sup> Intimacy is a "close, relaxed; and frank relationship between two or more individuals" that results from the seclusion of a pair or small group of individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-93">[93]</a></sup> Anonymity is the "desire of individuals for times of 'public privacy.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-93">[93]</a></sup> Lastly, reserve is the "creation of a psychological barrier against unwanted intrusion"; this creation of a psychological barrier requires others to respect an individual's need or desire to restrict communication of information concerning themself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the psychological barrier of reserve, Kirsty Hughes identified three more kinds of privacy barriers: physical, behavioral, and normative. Physical barriers, such as walls and doors, prevent others from accessing and experiencing the individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[94]</a></sup> (In this sense, "accessing" an individual includes accessing personal information about them.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[94]</a></sup> Behavioral barriers communicate to others—verbally, through language, or non-verbally, through personal space, body language, or clothing—that an individual does not want the other person to access or experience them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[94]</a></sup> Lastly, normative barriers, such as laws and social norms, restrain others from attempting to access or experience an individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secrecy">Secrecy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Secrecy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Privacy is sometimes defined as an option to have secrecy. Richard Posner said that privacy is the right of people to "conceal information about themselves that others might use to their disadvantage".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201021_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201021-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>In various legal contexts, when privacy is described as secrecy, a conclusion is reached: if privacy is secrecy, then rights to privacy do not apply for any information which is already publicly disclosed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201022–23_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201022–23-97">[97]</a></sup> When privacy-as-secrecy is discussed, it is usually imagined to be a selective kind of secrecy in which individuals keep some information secret and private while they choose to make other information public and not private.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201022–23_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201022–23-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personhood_and_autonomy">Personhood and autonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Personhood and autonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Privacy may be understood as a necessary precondition for the development and preservation of personhood. Jeffrey Reiman defined privacy in terms of a recognition of one's ownership of their physical and mental reality and a moral right to self-determination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-98">[98]</a></sup> Through the "social ritual" of privacy, or the social practice of respecting an individual's privacy barriers, the social group communicates to developing children that they have exclusive moral rights to their bodies—in other words, moral ownership of their body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-98">[98]</a></sup> This entails control over both active (physical) and cognitive appropriation, the former being control over one's movements and actions and the latter being control over who can experience one's physical existence and when.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alternatively, Stanley Benn defined privacy in terms of a recognition of oneself as a subject with agency—as an individual with the capacity to choose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-99">[99]</a></sup> Privacy is required to exercise choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-99">[99]</a></sup> Overt observation makes the individual aware of himself or herself as an object with a "determinate character" and "limited probabilities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-99">[99]</a></sup> Covert observation, on the other hand, changes the conditions in which the individual is exercising choice without his or her knowledge and consent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, privacy may be viewed as a state that enables autonomy, a concept closely connected to that of personhood. According to Joseph Kufer, an autonomous self-concept entails a conception of oneself as a "purposeful, self-determining, responsible agent" and an awareness of one's capacity to control the boundary between self and other—that is, to control who can access and experience him or her and to what extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-100">[100]</a></sup> Furthermore, others must acknowledge and respect the self's boundaries—in other words, they must respect the individual's privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>The studies of psychologists such as Jean Piaget and Victor Tausk show that, as children learn that they can control who can access and experience them and to what extent, they develop an autonomous self-concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-100">[100]</a></sup> In addition, studies of adults in particular institutions, such as Erving Goffman's study of "total institutions" such as prisons and mental institutions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> suggest that systemic and routinized deprivations or violations of privacy deteriorate one's sense of autonomy over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_100-3"><a href="#cite_note-:4-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-identity_and_personal_growth">Self-identity and personal growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Self-identity and personal growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Privacy may be understood as a prerequisite for the development of a sense of self-identity. Privacy barriers, in particular, are instrumental in this process. According to Irwin Altman, such barriers "define and limit the boundaries of the self" and thus "serve to help define [the self]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-102">[102]</a></sup> This control primarily entails the ability to regulate contact with others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-102">[102]</a></sup> Control over the "permeability" of the self's boundaries enables one to control what constitutes the self and thus to define what is the self.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-:5-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, privacy may be seen as a state that fosters personal growth, a process integral to the development of self-identity. Hyman Gross suggested that, without privacy—solitude, anonymity, and temporary releases from social roles—individuals would be unable to freely express themselves and to engage in self-discovery and <a href="/wiki/Self-criticism" title="Self-criticism">self-criticism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_100-4"><a href="#cite_note-:4-100">[100]</a></sup> Such self-discovery and self-criticism contributes to one's understanding of oneself and shapes one's sense of identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_100-5"><a href="#cite_note-:4-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intimacy">Intimacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Intimacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In a way analogous to how the personhood theory imagines privacy as some essential part of being an individual, the intimacy theory imagines privacy to be an essential part of the way that humans have strengthened or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intimate_relationships" title="Intimate relationships">intimate relationships</a> with other humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201035_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201035-103">[103]</a></sup> Because part of <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_relationship" title="Interpersonal relationship">human relationships</a> includes individuals volunteering to self-disclose most if not all personal information, this is one area in which privacy does not apply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201035_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201035-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/James_Rachels" title="James Rachels">James Rachels</a> advanced this notion by writing that privacy matters because "there is a close connection between our ability to control who has access to us and to information about us, and our ability to create and maintain different sorts of social relationships with different people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove201035_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove201035-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Protecting intimacy is at the core of the concept of sexual privacy, which law professor Danielle Citron argues should be protected as a unique form of privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_privacy">Physical privacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Physical privacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Physical privacy could be defined as preventing "intrusions into one's physical space or solitude."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> An example of the legal basis for the right to physical privacy is the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fourth Amendment</a>, which guarantees "the right of the people to be secure in their persons, houses, papers, and effects, against unreasonable searches and seizures".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Physical privacy may be a matter of cultural sensitivity, personal dignity, and/or shyness. There may also be concerns about safety, if, for example one is wary of becoming the victim of crime or <a href="/wiki/Stalking" title="Stalking">stalking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> There are different things that can be prevented to protect one's physical privacy, including people watching (even through recorded images) one's intimate behaviours or <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">body</a> parts and unhautorized access to one's personal possessions or places. Examples of possible efforts used to avoid the former, especially for <a href="/wiki/Modesty" title="Modesty">modesty</a> reasons, are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clothes" title="Clothes">clothes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wall" title="Wall">walls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fence" title="Fence">fences</a>, privacy screens, <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_glass" title="Cathedral glass">cathedral glass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Window_covering" title="Window covering">window coverings</a>, etc.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organizational">Organizational</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Organizational">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Government agencies, corporations, groups/societies and other organizations may desire to keep their activities or secrets from being revealed to other organizations or individuals, adopting various <a href="/wiki/Security" title="Security">security</a> practices and controls in order to keep private information confidential. Organizations may seek legal protection for their secrets. For example, a government administration may be able to invoke <a href="/wiki/Executive_privilege" title="Executive privilege">executive privilege</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> or declare certain information to be <a href="/wiki/Classified_information" title="Classified information">classified</a>, or a corporation might attempt to protect valuable proprietary information as <a href="/wiki/Trade_secret" title="Trade secret">trade secrets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Privacy_self-synchronization">Privacy self-synchronization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Privacy self-synchronization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Privacy self-synchronization is a hypothesized mode by which the stakeholders of an enterprise privacy program spontaneously contribute collaboratively to the program's maximum success. The stakeholders may be customers, employees, managers, executives, suppliers, partners or investors. When self-synchronization is reached, the model states that the personal interests of individuals toward their privacy is in balance with the business interests of enterprises who collect and use the personal information of those individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="An_individual_right">An individual right</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: An individual right">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>David Flaherty believes networked computer databases pose threats to privacy. He develops 'data protection' as an aspect of privacy, which involves "the collection, use, and dissemination of personal information". This concept forms the foundation for fair information practices used by governments globally. Flaherty forwards an idea of privacy as information control, "[i]ndividuals want to be left alone and to exercise some control over how information about them is used".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Richard Posner and Lawrence Lessig focus on the economic aspects of personal information control. Posner criticizes privacy for concealing information, which reduces market efficiency. For Posner, employment is selling oneself in the labour market, which he believes is like selling a product. Any 'defect' in the 'product' that is not reported is fraud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> For Lessig, privacy breaches online can be regulated through code and law. Lessig claims "the protection of privacy would be stronger if people conceived of the right as a property right",<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELessig2006p._229:_"In_my_view,_the_protection_of_privacy_would_be_stronger_if_people_conceived_of_the_right_as_a_property_right."_113-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELessig2006p._229:_"In_my_view,_the_protection_of_privacy_would_be_stronger_if_people_conceived_of_the_right_as_a_property_right."-113'>[113]</a></sup> and that "individuals should be able to control information about themselves".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELessig2006_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELessig2006-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A_collective_value_and_a_human_right">A collective value and a human right</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: A collective value and a human right">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There have been attempts to establish privacy as one of the fundamental <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>, whose social value is an essential component in the functioning of democratic societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priscilla Regan believes that individual concepts of privacy have failed philosophically and in policy. She supports a social value of privacy with three dimensions: shared perceptions, public values, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collectivism_and_individualism" title="Collectivism and individualism">collective</a> components. Shared ideas about privacy allows freedom of conscience and diversity in thought. Public values guarantee democratic participation, including freedoms of speech and association, and limits government power. Collective elements describe privacy as collective good that cannot be divided. Regan's goal is to strengthen privacy claims in policy making: "if we did recognize the collective or public-good value of privacy, as well as the common and public value of privacy, those advocating privacy protections would have a stronger basis upon which to argue for its protection".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Leslie Regan Shade argues that the human right to privacy is necessary for meaningful democratic participation, and ensures human dignity and autonomy. Privacy depends on norms for how information is distributed, and if this is appropriate. Violations of privacy depend on context. The human right to privacy has precedent in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="United Nations Declaration of Human Rights">United Nations Declaration of Human Rights</a>: "Everyone has the right to freedom of opinion and expression; this right includes freedom to hold opinions without interference and to seek, receive and impart information and ideas through any media and regardless of frontiers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Shade believes that privacy must be approached from a people-centered perspective, and not through the marketplace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dr. Eliza Watt, Westminster Law School, University of Westminster in London, UK, proposes application of the International Human Right Law (IHRL) concept of “virtual control” as an approach to deal with extraterritorial mass surveillance by state intelligence agencies.Dr. Watt envisions the “virtual control” test, understood as a remote control over the individual's right to privacy of communications, where privacy is recognized under the ICCPR, Article 17. This, she contends, may help to close the normative gap that is being exploited by nation states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Privacy_paradox_and_economic_valuation">Privacy paradox and economic valuation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Privacy paradox and economic valuation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>privacy paradox</i> is a phenomenon in which online users state that they are concerned about their privacy but behave as if they were not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swartz,_J._2000_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swartz,_J._2000-120">[120]</a></sup> While this term was coined as early as 1998,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> it wasn't used in its current popular sense until the year 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swartz,_J._2000_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Swartz,_J._2000-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Susan B. Barnes similarly used the term <i>privacy paradox</i> to refer to the ambiguous boundary between private and public space on social media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> When compared to adults, young people tend to disclose more information on <a href="#Social_Media">social media</a>. However, this does not mean that they are not concerned about their privacy. Susan B. Barnes gave a case in her article: in a television interview about Facebook, a student addressed her concerns about disclosing personal information online. However, when the reporter asked to see her Facebook page, she put her home address, phone numbers, and pictures of her young son on the page.
</p><p>The privacy paradox has been studied and scripted in different research settings. Several studies have shown this inconsistency between privacy attitudes and behavior among online users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> However, by now an increasing number of studies have also shown that there are significant and at times large correlations between privacy concerns and information sharing behavior,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> which speaks against the privacy paradox. A meta-analysis of 166 studies published on the topic reported an overall small but significant relation between privacy concerns and informations sharing or use of privacy protection measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> So although there are several individual instances or anecdotes where behavior appear paradoxical, on average privacy concerns and privacy behaviors seem to be related, and several findings question the general existence of the privacy paradox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the relationship between concerns and behavior is likely only small, and there are several arguments that can explain why that is the case. According to the <a href="/wiki/Value-action_gap" title="Value-action gap">attitude-behavior gap</a>, attitudes and behaviors are <i>in general</i> and in most cases not closely related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> A main explanation for the partial mismatch in the context of privacy specifically is that users lack awareness of the risks and the degree of protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Users may underestimate the harm of disclosing information online. On the other hand, some researchers argue that the mismatch comes from lack of technology literacy and from the design of sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>  For example, users may not know how to change their <a href="/wiki/Privacy_settings" title="Privacy settings">default settings</a> even though they care about their privacy. Psychologists Sonja Utz and Nicole C. Krämer particularly pointed out that the privacy paradox can occur when users must trade-off between their privacy concerns and impression management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Personal data have different personal and public value and are used for different purposes in citizen-state interactions, compared to customer-business interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-:10-132">[132]</a></sup> Therefore, the privacy paradox in citizen state actions – for example in AI driven public services – urges a broader public debate on how governments can safeguard citizen privacy, beyond the economic valuation of personal data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-:10-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_on_irrational_decision_making">Research on irrational decision making</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Research on irrational decision making">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="#Social_networking">§ Social networking</a>, and <a href="#Advertising_on_Mobile_Devices">§ Advertising on Mobile Devices</a></div>
<p>A study conducted by Susanne Barth and Menno D.T. de Jo demonstrates that decision making takes place on an irrational level, especially when it comes to mobile computing. Mobile applications in particular are often built up in such a way that spurs decision making that is fast and automatic without assessing risk factors. Protection measures against these unconscious mechanisms are often difficult to access while downloading and installing apps. Even with mechanisms in place to protect user privacy, users may not have the knowledge or experience to enable these mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barth_1038–1058_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barth_1038–1058-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Users of mobile applications generally have very little knowledge of how their personal data are used. When they decide which application to download, they typically do not rely on the information provided by application vendors regarding the collection and use of personal data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kokolakis_122–134_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kokolakis_122–134-134">[134]</a></sup> Other research finds that users are much more likely to be swayed by cost, functionality, design, ratings, reviews and number of downloads than requested permissions, regardless of how important users may claim permissions to be when asked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>A study by Zafeiropoulou specifically examined location data, which is a form of personal information increasingly used by mobile applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Their survey also found evidence that supports the existence of privacy paradox for location data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kokolakis_122–134_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kokolakis_122–134-134">[134]</a></sup>  Privacy risk perception in relation to the use of <a href="/wiki/Privacy-enhancing_technologies" title="Privacy-enhancing technologies">privacy-enhancing technologies</a> survey data indicates that a high perception of privacy risk is an insufficient motivator for people to adopt privacy protecting strategies, while knowing they exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kokolakis_122–134_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kokolakis_122–134-134">[134]</a></sup> It also raises a question on what the value of data is, as there is no equivalent of a stock-market for personal information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_economic_valuation_of_privacy">The economic valuation of privacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: The economic valuation of privacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The willingness to incur a privacy risk is suspected to be driven by a complex array of factors including risk attitudes, personal value for private information, and general attitudes to privacy (which may be derived from surveys).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frik_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frik-138">[138]</a></sup> One experiment aiming to determine the monetary value of several types of personal information indicated relatively low evaluations of personal information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kokolakis_122–134_134-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kokolakis_122–134-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Information_asymmetry">Information asymmetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Information asymmetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="#User_empowerment">§ User empowerment</a></div>
<p>Users are not always given the tools to live up to their professed privacy concerns, and they are sometimes willing to trade private information for convenience, functionality, or financial gain, even when the gains are very small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> One study suggests that people think their browser history is worth the equivalent of a cheap meal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2._The_Privacy_Paradox_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-2._The_Privacy_Paradox-140">[140]</a></sup> Another finds that attitudes to privacy risk do not appear to depend on whether it is already under threat or not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frik_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frik-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Inherent_necessity_for_privacy_violation">Inherent necessity for privacy violation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Inherent necessity for privacy violation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h6>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Privacy_concerns_with_social_networking_services" title="Privacy concerns with social networking services">Privacy concerns with social networking services</a></div>
<p>It is suggested by Andréa Belliger and David J. Krieger that the privacy paradox should not be considered a paradox, but more of a <i>privacy dilemma</i>, for services that cannot exist without the user sharing private data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2._The_Privacy_Paradox_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-2._The_Privacy_Paradox-140">[140]</a></sup> However, the general public is typically not given the choice whether to share private data or not,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloomberg-siri-alexa-listen_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg-siri-alexa-listen-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-state-arizona-redacted-complaint_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-state-arizona-redacted-complaint-52">[52]</a></sup> making it difficult to verify any claim that a service truly cannot exist without sharing private data.
</p>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Privacy_Calculus">Privacy Calculus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Privacy Calculus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h6>
<p>The privacy calculus model posits that two factors determine privacy behavior, namely privacy concerns (or perceived risks) and expected benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> By now, the privacy calculus was supported by several studies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> and it stands in direct contrast to the privacy paradox. Both perspectives can be consoled if they are understood from a more moderate position: Behavior is neither completely paradoxical nor completely logical, and the consistency between concerns and behavior depends on users, situations, or contexts.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Actions_which_reduce_privacy">Actions which reduce privacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Actions which reduce privacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As with other <a href="#Conceptions_of_privacy">conceptions of privacy</a>, there are various ways to discuss what kinds of processes or actions remove, challenge, lessen, or attack privacy. In 1960 legal scholar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Prosser_(academic)" title="William Prosser (academic)">William Prosser</a> created the following list of activities which can be remedied with privacy protection:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010101_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010101-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Intrusion into a person's private space, own affairs, or wish for solitude<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010101_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010101-145">[145]</a></sup></li>
<li>Public disclosure of personal information about a person which could be embarrassing for them to have revealed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010101_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010101-145">[145]</a></sup></li>
<li>Promoting access to information about a person which could lead the public to have incorrect beliefs about them<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010101_145-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010101-145">[145]</a></sup></li>
<li>Encroaching someone's personality rights, and using their likeness to advance interests which are not their own<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010101_145-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010101-145">[145]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>From 2004 to 2008, building from this and other historical precedents, Daniel J. Solove presented another classification of actions which are harmful to privacy, including collection of information which is already somewhat public, processing of information, sharing information, and invading personal space to get private information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010103_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010103-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collecting_information">Collecting information</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Collecting information">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the context of harming privacy, information collection means gathering whatever information can be obtained by doing something to obtain it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010103_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010103-147">[147]</a></sup> Examples include surveillance and <a href="/wiki/Interrogation" title="Interrogation">interrogation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010103_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010103-147">[147]</a></sup> Another example is how consumers and marketers also collect information in the business context through facial recognition which has recently caused a concern for things such as privacy. There is currently research being done related to this topic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aggregating_information">Aggregating information</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Aggregating information">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It can happen that privacy is not harmed when information is available, but that the harm can come when that information is collected as a set, then processed together in such a way that the collective reporting of pieces of information encroaches on privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup> Actions in this category which can lessen privacy include the following:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Data_aggregation" title="Data aggregation">data aggregation</a>, which is connecting many related but unconnected pieces of information<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup></li>
<li>identification, which can mean breaking the <a href="/wiki/De-identification" title="De-identification">de-identification</a> of items of data by putting it through a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De-anonymization" title="De-anonymization">de-anonymization</a> process, thus making facts which were intended to not name particular people to become associated with those people<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup></li>
<li>insecurity, such as lack of <a href="/wiki/Data_security" title="Data security">data security</a>, which includes when an organization is supposed to be responsible for protecting data instead suffers a <a href="/wiki/Data_breach" title="Data breach">data breach</a> which harms the people whose data it held<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup></li>
<li>secondary use, which is when people agree to share their data for a certain purpose, but then the data is used in ways without the data donors’ <a href="/wiki/Informed_consent" title="Informed consent">informed consent</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup></li>
<li>exclusion is the use of a person's data without any attempt to give the person an opportunity to manage the data or participate in its usage<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Information_dissemination">Information dissemination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Information dissemination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><i>Count not him among your friends who will retail your privacies to the world.</i></p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Publilius_Syrus" title="Publilius Syrus">Publilius Syrus</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Information dissemination is an attack on privacy when information which was shared in confidence is shared or threatened to be shared in a way that harms the subject of the information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are various examples of this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup> Breach of confidentiality is when one entity promises to keep a person's information private, then breaks that promise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup> Disclosure is making information about a person more accessible in a way that harms the subject of the information, regardless of how the information was collected or the intent of making it available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup> Exposure is a special type of disclosure in which the information disclosed is emotional to the subject or taboo to share, such as revealing their private life experiences, their nudity, or perhaps private body functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-11"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup> Increased accessibility means advertising the availability of information without actually distributing it, as in the case of doxxing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-12"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup> Blackmail is making a threat to share information, perhaps as part of an effort to coerce someone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-13"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup> Appropriation is an attack on the <a href="/wiki/Personhood" title="Personhood">personhood</a> of someone, and can include using the value of someone's reputation or likeness to advance interests which are not those of the person being appropriated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-14"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup> Distortion is the creation of misleading information or lies about a person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-15"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasion">Invasion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Invasion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Invasion of privacy, a subset of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expectation_of_privacy" title="Expectation of privacy">expectation of privacy</a>, is a different concept from the collecting, aggregating, and disseminating information because those three are a misuse of available data, whereas invasion is an attack on the right of individuals to keep personal secrets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-16"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup> An invasion is an attack in which information, whether intended to be public or not, is captured in a way that insults the personal dignity and right to private space of the person whose data is taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-17"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intrusion">Intrusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Intrusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>An <i>intrusion</i> is any unwanted entry into a person's private personal space and solitude for any reason, regardless of whether data is taken during that breach of space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-18"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup> <i>Decisional interference</i> is when an entity somehow injects itself into the personal decision-making process of another person, perhaps to influence that person's private decisions but in any case doing so in a way that disrupts the private personal thoughts that a person has.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05_149-19"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolove2010104–05-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples_of_invasions_of_privacy">Examples of invasions of privacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Examples of invasions of privacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>In 2019, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_contractors" title="Independent contractors">contract workers</a> for <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amazon_(company)" title="Amazon (company)">Amazon</a> reported being forced to continue listening to "<a href="#Intimacy">intimate</a> moments" captured on the companies' smart speakers in order to improve the quality of their automated speech recognition software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloomberg-siri-alexa-listen_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg-siri-alexa-listen-11">[11]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Techniques_to_improve_privacy">Techniques to improve privacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Techniques to improve privacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Privacy-enhancing_technologies" title="Privacy-enhancing technologies">Privacy-enhancing technologies</a></div>
<p>Similarly to <a href="#Actions_which_reduce_privacy">actions which reduce privacy</a>, there are multiple angles of privacy and multiple techniques to improve them to varying extents. When actions are done at an <a href="#Organizational">organizational level</a>, they may be referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cybersecurity" title="Cybersecurity">cybersecurity</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Encryption">Encryption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Encryption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Encryption" title="Encryption">Encryption</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pretty_Good_Privacy" title="Pretty Good Privacy">Pretty Good Privacy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Signal_(software)" title="Signal (software)">Signal (software)</a></div>
<p>Individuals can encrypt e-mails via enabling either two encryption protocols, <a href="/wiki/S/MIME" title="S/MIME">S/MIME</a>, which is built into companies like Apple or <a href="/wiki/Outlook.com" title="Outlook.com">Outlook</a> and thus most common, or <a href="/wiki/Pretty_Good_Privacy" title="Pretty Good Privacy">PGP</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Signal_(software)" title="Signal (software)">Signal</a> messaging app, which encrypts messages so that only the recipient can read the message, is notable for being available on many mobile devices and implementing a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfect_forward_secrecy" title="Perfect forward secrecy">perfect forward secrecy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anonymity">Anonymity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Anonymity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anonymity" title="Anonymity">Anonymity</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Anonymous_proxy" title="Anonymous proxy">Anonymizing proxies</a> or anonymizing networks like <a href="/wiki/I2P" title="I2P">I2P</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tor_(network)" title="Tor (network)">Tor</a> can be used to prevent Internet service providers (ISP) from knowing which sites one visits and with whom one communicates, by hiding IP addresses and location, but does not necessarily protect a user from third party data mining. Anonymizing proxies are built into a user's device, in comparison to a <a href="/wiki/Virtual_private_network" title="Virtual private network">Virtual Private Network</a> (VPN), where users must download software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Using a VPN hides all data and connections that are exchanged between servers and a user's computer, resulting in the online data of the user being unshared and secure, providing a barrier between the user and their ISP, and is especially important to use when a user is connected to public Wi-Fi. However, users should understand that all their data does flow through the VPN's servers rather than the ISP. Users should decide for themselves if they wish to use either an anonymizing proxy or a VPN.
</p><p>In a more non-technical sense, using incognito mode or private browsing mode will prevent a user's computer from saving history, Internet files, and cookies, but the ISP will still have access to the users' search history. Using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anonymous_web_browsing" title="Anonymous web browsing">anonymous search engines</a> will not share a user's history, clicks, and will obstruct ad blockers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-:9-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="User_empowerment">User empowerment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: User empowerment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/End_user" title="End user">End user</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="#Privacy_paradox_and_economic_valuation">§ Privacy paradox and economic valuation</a></div>
<p>Concrete solutions on how to solve paradoxical behavior still do not exist. Many efforts are focused on processes of decision making, like restricting data access permissions during application installation, but this would not completely bridge the gap between user intention and behavior. Susanne Barth and Menno D.T. de Jong believe that for users to make more conscious decisions on privacy matters, the design needs to be more user-oriented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barth_1038–1058_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barth_1038–1058-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_security_measures">Other security measures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Other security measures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In a social sense, simply limiting the amount of personal information that users posts on social media could increase their security, which in turn makes it harder for criminals to perform identity theft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-:9-153">[153]</a></sup>  Moreover, creating a set of complex passwords and using two-factor authentication can allow users to be less susceptible to their accounts being compromised when various data leaks occur. Furthermore, users should protect their digital privacy by using anti-virus software, which can block harmful viruses like a pop-up scanning for personal information on a users' computer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_methods">Legal methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Legal methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although there are laws that promote the protection of users, in some countries, like the U.S., there is no federal digital privacy law and privacy settings are essentially limited by the state of current enacted privacy laws. To further their privacy, users can start conversing with representatives, letting representatives know that privacy is a main concern, which in turn increases the likelihood of further privacy laws being enacted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Privacy_in_non-human_animals">Privacy in non-human animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Privacy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Privacy in non-human animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/David_Attenborough" title="David Attenborough">David Attenborough</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Biologist" title="Biologist">biologist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_historian" title="Natural historian">natural historian</a>, affirmed that gorillas "value their privacy" while discussing a brief escape by a gorilla in <a href="/wiki/London_Zoo" title="London Zoo">London Zoo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>Animals in zoos have been found to exhibit harmful or different behaviours due to the presence of visitors watching them:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WiredAnimal_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-WiredAnimal-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cotton-top_tamarins" title="Cotton-top tamarins">Cotton-top tamarins</a> in zoos engage in less social behaviours, including physical contact and sex, than ones in off-exhibit buildings.</li>
<li>Chimpanzees become more aggressive towards each other.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lion-tailed_macaque" title="Lion-tailed macaque">Lion-tailed macaques</a> pace and <a href="/wiki/Self-harm" title="Self-harm">bite themselves</a> more in direct proportions to human visitors.</li>
<li>In one zoo, orangutans have been shown to cover their heads less as the density of visitors decreased.</li></ul>
<p>Some studies have found in the contrary that being watched does not change non-human animals' behaviours, including covering faces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WiredAnimal_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-WiredAnimal-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
